This is the difference between knowing and doing — and Islam consistently values the latter.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. For each skill, define a "proof of competence" — a specific deliverable that would be impossible at your current level. Examples: a completed project, a presentation, a passed exam, or a successful client engagement.<br>2. Set a date for completing this proof — ideally at the end of your 90-day plan.<br>3. Share your goal with your accountability partner, mentor, or manager so there is external expectation.<br>4. At the 90-day mark, complete the deliverable or take the assessment.<br>5. Evaluate honestly: Did you close the gap? Partially? If not fully, what remains? Update your plan for the next quarter accordingly. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Core | Ensure your primary income source is fully halal — audit contracts, clients, and products | Review your employment contract or business model for any haram elements (riba, gharar, prohibited products)<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Your employment contract or business model is the foundation of your income. If it contains elements of riba (interest), gharar (excessive uncertainty/deception), or involvement with prohibited products (alcohol, gambling, etc.), then everything built on that foundation is tainted. This is not about being overly cautious — it is about protecting the purity of your rizq (provision) at its source.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Obtain a full copy of your employment contract, partnership agreement, or business registration documents.<br>2. Read every clause with these questions in mind: Does any part of my compensation involve interest? Do I sell, produce, or facilitate anything explicitly prohibited in Islam? Are there penalty clauses or structures that function as riba?<br>3. If you are a business owner, map your revenue streams: where does every pound come from, and what does the customer receive in return?<br>4. Flag any clauses or revenue streams you are unsure about — do not dismiss grey areas.<br>5. Document your findings in a simple audit sheet: item, concern, status (clear / grey / problematic). | — |
| Intellect | professional | Core | Ensure your primary income source is fully halal — audit contracts, clients, and products | Audit your client list — are any clients in industries that are impermissible?<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Even if your own work is halal in nature, providing services to clients whose core business is haram makes you a facilitator of that harm. The principle of ta'awun (cooperation) in the Quran instructs believers to cooperate in righteousness and not in sin and transgression. Auditing your client list ensures your professional relationships align with this principle.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. List every client, customer, or account you currently serve.<br>2. For each client, identify their core business: what do they sell or produce? How do they make money?<br>3. Flag any client whose primary revenue comes from prohibited industries: conventional finance (interest-based), alcohol, gambling, tobacco, adult entertainment, or weapons manufacturing.<br>4. For borderline cases (e.g., a conglomerate with both halal and haram divisions), assess whether your specific work supports the halal or haram side of their business.<br>5. Prepare a plan for transitioning away from problematic clients — this may require finding replacement revenue first, but the intention and timeline should be set now. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Core | Ensure your primary income source is fully halal — audit contracts, clients, and products | Check your investment and savings vehicles for interest-based components<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Many professionals have savings accounts, pension funds, or investment portfolios that generate interest (riba) without their active awareness. Riba is among the most severely warned-against sins in the Quran. Checking your savings and investments ensures that the money you set aside for the future is growing through permissible means, not accumulating sin alongside returns.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. List every account where your money sits: savings accounts, current accounts, pension funds, ISAs, investment portfolios, and any other financial instruments.<br>2. For each account, determine: does it pay or charge interest? Is it invested in interest-bearing bonds or prohibited industries?<br>3. Contact your pension provider and request the fund's investment breakdown — many default pension funds include interest-based bonds and shares in prohibited companies.<br>4. Research Islamic alternatives: Islamic banks, Shariah-compliant pension funds, halal investment platforms (e.g., Wahed Invest, Islamicly).<br>5. Create a transition plan with specific dates for moving each account to a halal alternative. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Core | Ensure your primary income source is fully halal — audit contracts, clients, and products | Consult a knowledgeable scholar if any grey areas arise<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Not every financial question has a clear-cut answer. Islamic commercial law (fiqh al-mu'amalat) is a deep and nuanced field, and applying it to modern professional contexts requires expertise most of us do not have. Consulting a knowledgeable scholar for grey areas is not a sign of weakness — it is a sign of taqwa (God-consciousness) and intellectual honesty.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Compile your list of grey areas from the previous subtasks — specific clauses, client relationships, or financial products you are unsure about.<br>2. Identify a scholar who has expertise in Islamic commercial law (fiqh al-mu'amalat), not just general Islamic knowledge. Ask your local mosque, Islamic finance professionals, or trusted community members for recommendations.<br>3. Prepare your questions in writing with full context: describe the contract clause, business relationship, or financial product in detail so the scholar can give an informed ruling.<br>4. Present the facts honestly — do not frame questions to get the answer you want.<br>5. If the scholar's guidance requires changes, document the ruling and your action plan. If you receive conflicting opinions, follow the one that is most cautious (the principle of wara'). | — |
| Intellect | professional | Core | Ensure your primary income source is fully halal — audit contracts, clients, and products | Create a plan to transition away from any problematic income sources<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Identifying haram income sources is only valuable if you act on the findings. Allah judges by intention and effort, and a sincere plan with a firm deadline demonstrates both.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Can you switch to a Shariah-compliant financial product without significant loss?<br>5. Execute the transition in order of severity. Begin with the most clearly problematic source, even if it is also the most financially significant — trust in Allah's provision (tawakkul) paired with practical effort. | — |

| Intellect | professional | Core | Build a professional portfolio or record of your best work | For each piece, write a brief description of the problem, your approach, and the outcome<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Raw work samples without context are difficult to evaluate. A hiring manager, client, or collaborator needs to understand not just what you produced, but why it mattered and how you approached it. The problem-approach-outcome structure tells a story that makes your competence legible to others and demonstrates your ability to think, not just execute.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. For each portfolio piece, write three short paragraphs (or bullet points):<br>   - **Problem:** What was the challenge or need? Why did it matter?<br>   - **Approach:** What did you do? What decisions did you make and why?<br>   - **Outcome:** What was the result? Use numbers where possible (revenue generated, time saved, users served).<br>2. Keep each description under 200 words — concise and impactful.<br>3. Use plain language, not jargon. Your portfolio may be read by people outside your specialisation.<br>4. Have someone unfamiliar with the project read your description — if they can understand the value you created, you have written it well.<br>5. Include any relevant visuals: screenshots, diagrams, before/after comparisons. | — |

| Intellect | professional | Growth | Pursue advanced training, specialisation, or a higher qualification in your field | Calculate the time, cost, and opportunity cost of pursuing it<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Every investment has a cost — not just in money, but in time and what you give up by choosing this path over another. Distinguish between what you need a mentor for versus what you can learn from a course, book, or peer. Mentorship is most valuable for tacit knowledge — the things that cannot be learned from a textbook.<br>5. Be honest about your commitment level: how much time can you invest in the mentorship relationship per month? | — |

| Intellect | professional | Growth | Find a professional mentor — someone 5–10 years ahead on the path you want to walk | Prepare thoroughly for each meeting — never waste your mentor's time<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Your mentor is giving you their most valuable resource: time and attention. Thorough preparation maximises the value of every interaction, demonstrates your seriousness, and makes your mentor feel that their investment in you is worthwhile. This is adab (etiquette) in its most practical form.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Review them within 24 hours while the conversation is fresh.<br>5. Send a brief thank-you message after each meeting with a summary of your key takeaways and planned actions. This closes the loop and shows respect. | — |

Add all new contacts to your professional network tracker (CRM, spreadsheet, or contact notes) with context about where you met and what you discussed. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Growth | Develop a personal "board of advisors" — 3–5 trusted people who challenge your professional thinking | Identify 3-5 people whose professional judgement you deeply respect<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>The quality of advice you receive is determined entirely by the quality of the people you ask. Your advisory board should not be composed of people who will simply agree with you — they should be people whose professional judgement you trust enough to follow even when their advice is uncomfortable. This is the essence of shura: consultation that genuinely influences decisions.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Think about the people in your professional life whose opinions have changed your mind in the past. Who challenged an assumption and turned out to be right?<br>2. Consider people across different relationship types: a former manager, a respected peer, a client who gives brutally honest feedback, a professional from a different industry.<br>3. Assess each candidate on three dimensions: competence (do they know what they are talking about?), candour (will they tell you the truth, not what you want to hear?), and care (do they genuinely want to see you succeed?).<br>4. All three dimensions must be present. Competence without candour gives you polite but useless advice. Candour without care gives you harsh criticism without constructive direction.<br>5. Write down your top 5 candidates and the specific value each one brings to your thinking. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Growth | Develop a personal "board of advisors" — 3–5 trusted people who challenge your professional thinking | Ensure diversity — different industries, perspectives, strengths, and backgrounds<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>A board of advisors who all think alike is an echo chamber, not a shura council. Diversity of perspective is what makes collective wisdom greater than individual insight. The Quran describes the ummah as a community of different nations and tribes so that they may know one another — this principle of beneficial diversity applies directly to your advisory board.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Review your candidate list and map their backgrounds: industry, functional expertise, cultural background, age, and career stage.<br>2. Identify gaps: are all your candidates from the same industry? The same generation? The same worldview?<br>3. Actively seek out candidates who bring a missing dimension. If all your advisors are corporate professionals, consider adding an entrepreneur. If all are Muslim, consider adding a non-Muslim professional whose ethical compass you respect.<br>4. Include at least one person who is strong where you are weak — if you are a visionary thinker, include a detail-oriented operator; if you are technically gifted, include someone with strong people skills.<br>5. The goal is not diversity for its own sake but diversity that challenges your blind spots and broadens your perspective. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Growth | Develop a personal "board of advisors" — 3–5 trusted people who challenge your professional thinking | Invite each with a clear explanation of what you're building and what you're asking<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>People cannot help you effectively if they do not understand what you are asking. A clear invitation sets expectations, prevents misunderstandings, and demonstrates professionalism. It also honours the other person's time by letting them make an informed decision about whether to participate.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Craft a personalised invitation for each person that includes:<br>   - What you are building: "I'm assembling a small personal advisory board of 3-5 people I trust to challenge my professional thinking."<br>   - Why them specifically: "I deeply respect your [specific quality or expertise], and I believe your perspective would be invaluable because [specific reason]."<br>   - What you are asking: "A quarterly conversation of about 45-60 minutes where I share my plans and challenges, and you give me honest feedback."<br>   - What you are not asking: clarify that this is informal, unpaid, and low-commitment. No one should feel trapped.<br>2. Deliver the invitation in person or by personal message — not in a group email.<br>3. Give them time to respond. Do not pressure an immediate answer.<br>4. If they decline, thank them sincerely and maintain the relationship. Do not make it awkward.<br>5. When someone accepts, confirm the first meeting date immediately. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Growth | Develop a personal "board of advisors" — 3–5 trusted people who challenge your professional thinking | Schedule quarterly check-ins where you present your plans and receive candid feedback<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>The structure of regular check-ins transforms an informal advisory relationship into a disciplined practice of shura. Quarterly cadence is ideal: frequent enough to maintain continuity but infrequent enough to respect everyone's time. Presenting your plans formally — rather than just chatting — ensures the feedback is substantive and actionable.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Schedule the first round of meetings within the first month of forming the board. Coordinate schedules and choose a consistent time window (e.g., first week of each quarter).<br>2. Prepare a brief (1-2 page) quarterly update to share with each advisor before the meeting. Include: what you accomplished last quarter, what you plan for next quarter, and 2-3 specific questions or decisions you want their input on.<br>3. During the meeting, present your update in 10 minutes, then open the floor for questions and feedback for the remaining 30-45 minutes.<br>4. Take detailed notes. After the meeting, send a brief summary of the advice received and any actions you plan to take.<br>5. Track which advice you followed and what resulted — share these outcomes at the next meeting to close the feedback loop. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Growth | Develop a personal "board of advisors" — 3–5 trusted people who challenge your professional thinking | Reciprocate — offer your own expertise and perspective to each advisor<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>A one-sided advisory relationship becomes a burden. Even if your advisors are more senior or experienced than you, you have unique perspectives, skills, and information that can benefit them. Reciprocating creates a mutually valuable relationship that is sustainable long-term. The Prophet (peace be upon him) taught that the best relationships are those where both parties benefit.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. For each advisor, identify what you can uniquely offer: your expertise in a specific area, your knowledge of a different industry, your technical skills, your perspective as someone at a different career stage, or simply your fresh eyes on their challenges.<br>2. Proactively offer help: "Is there anything I can help you with? I'd love to reciprocate the value you bring to me."<br>3. Share relevant articles, introductions, or opportunities when you come across them — even outside of scheduled meetings.<br>4. Be attentive to their challenges: if an advisor mentions a problem you can help with, follow up with a concrete offer.<br>5. Express genuine gratitude regularly — not just at meetings, but when their advice proves valuable. Tell them the specific impact their guidance had. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Publish original research, a case study, or an innovation in your field | Identify a novel insight, solution, or finding from your professional work<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Every professional accumulates unique insights through their work, but few take the time to identify and articulate them. The knowledge sitting in your experience — a creative solution to a recurring problem, an unexpected finding, or a new approach that worked — has value beyond your immediate context. Identifying it is the first step to creating a contribution that benefits your entire field.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Review your past 1-2 years of professional work. Look for moments where you solved a problem in an unconventional way, discovered something surprising, or developed a new method.<br>2. Ask yourself: "What do I know from experience that most people in my field have to learn the hard way? What would I teach a junior professional that isn't in any textbook?"<br>3. Talk to colleagues and clients: "What do you think I do differently or particularly well?" — their answers often point to your unique contributions.<br>4. Look for patterns: if you have solved the same type of problem multiple times using a consistent approach, that approach may be worth documenting.<br>5. Select the insight with the broadest applicability — the one that would help the most people if they knew about it. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Publish original research, a case study, or an innovation in your field | Research existing literature to confirm your contribution is genuinely original<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Publishing something that already exists wastes your time and damages your credibility. A thorough literature review ensures your contribution is genuinely original — or, if similar work exists, that you can clearly articulate how yours differs, extends, or improves upon it. This rigour is part of intellectual amanah: being honest about what is truly new.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Search academic databases (Google Scholar, PubMed, IEEE, SSRN) and industry publications for work related to your insight.<br>2. Use multiple search terms — different authors may describe similar concepts with different terminology.<br>3. Read the abstracts and conclusions of the most relevant papers. If any closely match your insight, read them in full.<br>4. Ask yourself: "Has someone already said this? If so, what does my contribution add — new data, a different context, a better method, or a practical application?"<br>5. Document your literature review: a brief summary of what exists and a clear statement of how your contribution is different. This becomes the "literature review" section of your publication. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Publish original research, a case study, or an innovation in your field | Write the piece in a format appropriate for your field (journal article, case study, white paper)<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>The format of your publication must match the norms of your field and the expectations of your audience. A journal article carries weight in academia; a case study resonates with practitioners; a white paper reaches business decision-makers. Choosing the right format and writing to its conventions ensures your work is taken seriously and reaches the people who can benefit from it most.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Identify the format most respected in your field for the type of contribution you are making. Ask colleagues or your mentor for guidance.<br>2. Study 2-3 exemplary publications in that format — note their structure, length, tone, and level of detail.<br>3. Write your first draft following the standard structure:<br>   - **Journal article:** Abstract, Introduction, Literature Review, Methodology, Findings, Discussion, Conclusion.<br>   - **Case study:** Background, Challenge, Approach, Results, Lessons Learned.<br>   - **White paper:** Executive Summary, Problem Statement, Analysis, Recommendations, Conclusion.<br>4. Write the first draft without obsessing over perfection — get your ideas down, then refine.<br>5. Have a colleague review the draft for clarity, accuracy, and completeness before finalising. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Publish original research, a case study, or an innovation in your field | Submit to a reputable publication, conference, or platform<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>A piece of writing sitting on your hard drive benefits no one. Submitting to a reputable publication, conference, or platform is the act that transforms private knowledge into public contribution. It also subjects your work to peer review, which strengthens it. The act of submission requires courage — putting your ideas into the world for scrutiny — but this is how knowledge advances.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Research the most appropriate venues for your work: academic journals, industry magazines, conference proceedings, or professional platforms (Medium, LinkedIn articles, or industry-specific blogs).<br>2. Review the submission guidelines carefully — formatting, word count, citation style, and deadlines.<br>3. Tailor your piece to the specific venue: adjust the tone, depth, and emphasis to match their audience.<br>4. Submit according to their process. For peer-reviewed publications, be prepared for a review cycle of weeks to months.<br>5. If rejected, read the reviewer feedback carefully, revise, and submit to an alternative venue. Rejection is a normal part of the publishing process, not a judgement on your worth. | — |

If the piece generates discussion or questions, engage thoughtfully — this builds your reputation as a contributor, not just a publisher.<br>5. | Intellect | professional | Excellence | Take on a leadership or governance role — board position, committee chair, or department head | Assess your readiness — do you have the skills, time, and support to lead well?<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Taking on a leadership role you are not ready for harms both you and the people you are meant to serve. The Prophet (peace be upon him) warned against seeking authority one is not equipped to handle. Skills assessment: Does the role require skills you have (strategy, governance, people management, financial oversight)? Be specific — "leadership" is too vague.<br>2. Support assessment: Does your family understand and support this commitment? Does your employer permit it? Will it conflict with other obligations?<br>4. Seek external input: ask a trusted advisor, "Do you think I'm ready for this role? What gaps should I address first?"<br>5. If you identify readiness gaps, create a short plan to address them before applying. It is better to prepare for 3-6 months and lead well than to jump in unprepared. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Take on a leadership or governance role — board position, committee chair, or department head | Express your interest or apply for the role<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Many qualified professionals never lead because they wait to be asked. Expressing interest is not arrogance — it is responsibility. If you have assessed your readiness and believe you can serve well, stepping forward is an act of courage and service. If there is a selection process, treat it with the same rigour you would a job interview: research the organisation, understand their challenges, and articulate how you would address them.<br>5. If not selected, ask for feedback and express continued interest for future opportunities. Persistence without desperation is respected. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Take on a leadership or governance role — board position, committee chair, or department head | If appointed, establish clear goals for your tenure in the first 30 days<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>The first 30 days of any leadership role set the tone for your entire tenure. Leaders who begin without clear goals drift into reactive management — responding to whatever lands on their desk rather than driving intentional change. Establishing goals early creates accountability, builds confidence among those you lead, and ensures your limited time in the role produces lasting impact.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. In the first week, listen: meet with key stakeholders, review recent meeting minutes, and understand the current state of the team or organisation.<br>2. By day 15, identify the 2-3 most impactful goals you can realistically achieve during your tenure. These should be specific, measurable, and meaningful — not a laundry list.<br>3. Write a brief "tenure goals" document and share it with the people you will be working with. Invite feedback and adjust.<br>4. Identify quick wins — small improvements you can make in the first 30 days to build momentum and credibility.<br>5. Set quarterly milestones for each goal and schedule regular reviews. A goal without a check-in date is a wish. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Take on a leadership or governance role — board position, committee chair, or department head | Lead with the principles of shura, adl (justice), and ihsan (excellence)<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Islamic leadership is servant leadership: you are there to serve those you lead, not to accumulate authority. The three principles of shura (consultation), adl (justice), and ihsan (excellence) provide a complete framework for how to lead. Shura ensures wise decisions through collective input. Adl ensures fair treatment of everyone. Ihsan ensures that everything you do reflects the highest standard of quality and care.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. **Shura (consultation):** Before making any significant decision, consult the people it affects. Create structures for input: regular meetings, anonymous feedback channels, or one-on-one check-ins. Genuinely consider input — do not consult performatively.<br>2. **Adl (justice):** Apply rules and expectations consistently. Do not show favouritism, even to people you like or who support you. When conflicts arise, hear all sides before deciding. Document decisions and their reasoning for transparency.<br>3. **Ihsan (excellence):** Hold yourself to a higher standard than you hold others. Be the first to arrive and the last to leave (metaphorically). Deliver on your commitments. Pay attention to details that others overlook.<br>4. Regularly ask yourself: "Am I leading in a way I would be proud to present to Allah?" This is the ultimate accountability.<br>5. At the end of your tenure, seek honest feedback on your leadership and document lessons learned for your successor. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Design and deliver a training programme that uplifts others in your profession | Identify the most common skill gap or struggle among junior professionals in your field<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>The most impactful training programmes address real, widespread needs — not what you enjoy teaching, but what learners desperately need to learn. Starting with the most common struggle ensures your programme serves the greatest number of people and solves a genuine problem. This is the difference between a training programme that is interesting and one that is transformative.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Talk to junior professionals in your field: ask them directly, "What is the hardest part of your job? Where do you feel least prepared?"<br>2. Talk to managers and team leads: "What is the most common gap you see in junior hires? Where do they consistently struggle?"<br>3. Review online forums, subreddits, and professional communities for recurring questions and frustrations from early-career professionals.<br>4. Reflect on your own early career: what did you wish someone had taught you? What took you years to learn that could have taken weeks with the right guidance?<br>5. Identify the single most common and impactful gap — this is your training programme's focus. Resist the temptation to address everything; depth on one topic beats breadth across many. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Design and deliver a training programme that uplifts others in your profession | Design a curriculum that addresses this gap with clear learning outcomes<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>A curriculum without clear learning outcomes is a collection of topics, not a programme. Learning outcomes define what participants will be able to do after completing the training — not just what they will know. This outcome-focused design ensures every module, exercise, and minute of the programme serves a purpose and that success is measurable.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Write 3-5 specific learning outcomes using the format: "By the end of this programme, participants will be able to [specific, observable action]."<br>2. Work backwards from these outcomes: what knowledge, skills, and practice does a learner need to achieve each outcome?<br>3. Organise the content into a logical sequence of modules — each building on the previous one. A typical structure:<br>   - Module 1: Foundation (concepts and context)<br>   - Module 2-3: Core skills (the main content)<br>   - Module 4: Application (putting it all together in a realistic scenario)<br>4. For each module, estimate the time required and the teaching method (lecture, discussion, hands-on exercise, case study).<br>5. Build in assessment points: how will you know if participants are achieving the outcomes? Include at least one practical exercise per module. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Design and deliver a training programme that uplifts others in your profession | Create materials: slides, exercises, case studies, and assessments<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>Materials are the tangible delivery mechanism for your curriculum. High-quality materials allow participants to learn during the session and reference the content afterwards. They also allow the programme to be delivered by others in the future, extending its reach beyond your personal capacity. This is where your training programme becomes a lasting resource.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. For each module, create:<br>   - **Slides or notes:** Clear, visual, and concise. Avoid walls of text — use diagrams, examples, and key points.<br>   - **Exercises:** Hands-on activities that let participants practise the skill in a safe environment. Include clear instructions and expected outcomes.<br>   - **Case studies:** Real-world scenarios (anonymised if necessary) that illustrate the concepts in context.<br>   - **Assessments:** Short quizzes, practical assignments, or reflection questions that measure learning.<br>2. Write a facilitator guide: notes for anyone delivering the programme on timing, common questions, and how to handle different skill levels.<br>3. Create participant handouts: summaries, checklists, or reference sheets they can take away.<br>4. Ensure all materials are accessible: clear fonts, sufficient contrast, and available in formats participants can use.<br>5. Review all materials for accuracy, clarity, and coherence. Have a colleague review them with fresh eyes. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Design and deliver a training programme that uplifts others in your profession | Pilot the programme with a small cohort and gather detailed feedback<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>A training programme that has not been tested is a theory, not a product. Piloting with a small cohort reveals what works, what does not, and what you did not anticipate — insights that are impossible to gain from design alone. The pilot cohort's feedback is the most valuable input you will receive, and acting on it dramatically improves the programme before it reaches a larger audience.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Recruit 5-10 participants who match your target audience. Explain that this is a pilot and their honest feedback is essential.<br>2. Deliver the full programme as designed, but take detailed notes on what worked, what fell flat, and where participants struggled or excelled.<br>3. After each module, collect brief feedback: "What was most useful? What was confusing? What would you change?"<br>4. At the end of the programme, conduct a more thorough evaluation: a survey covering content quality, pacing, materials, exercises, and overall impact.<br>5. Ask the most candid question: "Would you recommend this programme to a colleague? If not, what would need to change?" | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Design and deliver a training programme that uplifts others in your profession | Iterate based on feedback and deliver to a larger audience<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>The gap between a pilot and a polished programme is closed by iteration. Taking pilot feedback seriously and making substantive changes — not just cosmetic tweaks — is what separates excellent training from mediocre training. This iterative mindset is itself a professional virtue: the willingness to improve based on evidence rather than defending your first draft.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Review all pilot feedback systematically. Categorise it: content issues, pacing issues, material quality, exercise effectiveness, and facilitation.<br>2. Prioritise changes by impact: fix the issues that affected the most participants or that undermined the learning outcomes most severely.<br>3. Revise materials, restructure modules, and adjust timing based on what you learned.<br>4. If possible, run one more small cohort with the revised programme to verify that your changes worked.<br>5. Once confident, deliver to a larger audience through your organisation, professional community, or a public platform. Consider partnering with a professional body or training organisation to extend your reach. | — |
| Intellect | professional | Excellence | Design and deliver a training programme that uplifts others in your profession | Make materials available as a lasting resource (open-source, community library, or online)<br><br>**Why does this matter?**<br><br>The greatest impact of a training programme comes not from the sessions you personally deliver, but from the materials that continue to teach after you stop. Making your materials freely available is a profound act of sadaqah jariyah — ongoing charity. Every person who learns from your materials, and every person they go on to help, adds to your account of good deeds. This is legacy in its truest Islamic sense.<br><br>---<br><br>**How do I accomplish this?**<br><br>1. Choose a distribution platform appropriate to your field: GitHub for technical materials, a personal website, a professional community library, or a platform like Coursera or Udemy.<br>2. License your materials appropriately: a Creative Commons licence (e.g., CC BY-SA) allows others to use and adapt your work while giving you credit.<br>3. Package the materials for self-study: add written instructions, reading orders, and self-assessment guides so people can use them without a facilitator.<br>4. Announce the resource through your professional network, relevant online communities, and your organisation.<br>5. Maintain the materials: update them annually to reflect new developments in your field. Invite community contributions if appropriate — let others improve what you started. | — |
